50 times a day. That's how often AI makes decisions for you. Your spam filter, your GPS, your Netflix queue, even your credit card. But 90% of people still think AI means killer robots. And it's surprisingly simple. AI is making machines solve problems the way humans do, recognizing images, understanding speech, making decisions, creating content. Think of it like cooking. Traditional software follows a recipe step by step. You tell the computer exactly what to do. But AI is different. You show it thousands of finished dishes and it writes its own recipe. You give it examples. It figures out the rules. That's it. That's AI. Machines that learn from examples instead of following instructions. Here's something most people don't realize. AI isn't new. The term artificial intelligence was coined in 1956 before the internet even existed. Alan Turing was asking whether machines could think back in 1950. For 70 years, AI quietly evolved in research labs. Deep learning arrived in 2012. The transformer architecture of the TNGPT was invented in 2017. Then in 2022, ChatgBD gave AI a face everyone could talk to. What feels brand new is actually just the interface. A prompt is just how you ask. Think of ordering food. Give me food gets you mystery meat. Grilled salmon, medium, lemon on the side gets exactly what you want. Be specific with AI. Get better answers. Next, token. That model has a 200k token limit. Tokens are how AI reads text like scrabble ties. It breaks words into small pieces. Hamburger becomes three tokens. Ham, burr, g. More tokens the AI can handle, more text it can work with at once. Hallucination. Be careful. AI hallucinates. You know that friend who tells incredible stories. Sounds totally convincing, but half the details are made up. They're not lying on purpose. They just fill in gaps with whatever sounds right. AI does the exact same thing. It can sound perfectly confident while being completely wrong. Always double check. Training data. It depends on what it was trained on. Imagine a chef who only ever tasted Italian food. It uses a deep neural network. Picture a mail sorting office with many floors. Ground floor sorts letters by size. Next floor sorts by destination. Next by priority. By the top floor, every letter lands in the right bin. Each floor is a layer. That's a neural network. Layers of sorting, each catching what the last one missed. Transformer. GPT stands for generative pre-trained transformer. Imagine the difference between a typewriter and a speedreader. Old AI read text one word at a time, left to right, like a typewriter. The transformer, invented in 2017, reads the whole page at once and instantly spots which words connect to each other, even across long paragraphs. That single invention made chatg possible. R A retrieval augmented generation. We added R A to reduce hallucinations. Picture two students taking an exam. One has a closed book, answering from memory, guessing when they don't know. The other has an open book. Looks up the answer first, then responds. Same student, much better answers. R A gives AI an open book. Context window. Claude has a 200K context window. Think of it as the size of AI's desk. A tiny desk fits one sticky note. A big desk fits stacks of documents. The context window is how much text AI can see at one time. Bigger window means it can read longer documents and remember more of your conversation. Last one, a GI, artificial general intelligence. Are we close to AGI? Today's AI is like a world champion chess player who can't make toast. Brilliant at one specific thing, clueless about everything else. A GI would be a human level generalist, good at everything. Research that used to take years now takes minutes. But here's what AI cannot do. It hallucinates. It confidently makes things up. Legal cases that don't exist. Historical events that never happened. It doesn't truly understand anything. It recognizes patterns incredibly well, but it has no idea what the words mean. And critically, it can't verify its own answers. It doesn't know when it's wrong. === Claude Code Just Became an AI Orchestration Platform! ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=V1lKbKnJRmQ

Six features, eight weeks. A complete paradigm shift. Parallel code review that fixes itself. One command that spawns 30 PRs. Coding from your phone. Sessions that teleport between cloud and terminal. This is the new Claude Code. Plus, stay until the end for free access to the Dynamis Claude Code latest features workshop recording. In 8 weeks, Claude Code shipped more paradigm shifting features than most tools ship in a year, and most developers missed all of them. January 7th, skills merge. Most developers are still using cloud code like a chatbot. Let me show you what actually changed. Here is the first feature you should try today. /simplify. You type one command. Three specialized agents launch in parallel. Agent one scans for code reuse, finding duplicated logic and utilities you already have but forgot about. Agent two checks code quality, readability, naming, and your claw MD rules. Agent three hunts efficiency problems, unnecessary loops, missed concurrency, expensive operations buried in tight iterations. All three run simultaneously on your recently changed files. When they finish, they do not just report problems. They fix them directly in your working copy. You're probably thinking that sounds like a llinter. It is not. Lint check syntax. SLSimplify reads your cla MD, understands your project conventions, and makes architectural code changes. It catches three to five real issues per feature branch that human reviewers typically miss. You can focus it too /simplify focus on memory efficiency /simplify check for error handling gaps. One gotcha, it modifies files directly. Always run get diff before you commit. This is not a suggestion. This is a rule. But that is not even the most interesting part. Flashbatch takes everything /simplify does and scales it across your entire codebase. Here is how it works. You type /batch replace all console.log log calls with structured logger. Phase one, claude researches your codebase, identifies every affected file, and decomposes the work into five to 30 independent units. It shows you the plan. Nothing happens until you approve. Phase two, after you say yes, one agent spawns per unit. Each agent gets its own isolated gitwork tree. They cannot interfere with each other. They implement run/simplify on their changes. Execute your test suite and open a pull request all in parallel. Phase three, a real-time status table updates as work is complete. 22 units decomposed, 22 PRs created. Each PR is independently reviewable and mergeable. No 500 file mega PRs. Here is the key insight. A weeklong migration becomes a parallelized reviewable process. Migrate from chest to vest. Convert CommonJS to ES imports at type annotations across 50 files. One command and every PR has already been through three agent code review. The catch units must be truly independent. If agent A creates a utility that agent B needs to import, it breaks. Create shared dependencies first, then batch the rest. Stop for a second. You might be wondering how 30 parallel agents can edit the same codebase without destroying each other's work. The answer is get work trees. Before work trees, parallel agents were limited to reading files or writing to non-over overlapping paths. Fragile. One collision and everything breaks. With native workree integration, each agent gets a completely isolated copy of your codebase. Agent A rewrites or.ts. Agent B rewrites the exact same file. Both work independently. You review both branches and pick the winner. Three levels of integration. The CLI flag claw-work tree feature off creates a named work tree instantly. Sub aent front matter with isolation colon workree makes any custom agent run in its own work tree automatically. The desktop app does it for every session by default. When an agent finishes with no changes, the work tree disappears automatically. Clean. No orphan branches cluttering your repo. Now, here is where it all comes together. Agent teams is the coordination layer that makes parallel work intelligent, not just parallel. One session acts as team lead. It spawns teammates, each with their own full context window. Here is the difference from sub agents. Subagents report back to the main agent and cannot talk to each other. If agent one discovers something agent two needs, the main agent has to relay it. Agent teams remove that bottleneck. Teammates message each other directly, share findings mid task, and self-coordinate through a shared task list. You're thinking, does this actually work at scale? Nicholas Khalini, an anthropic researcher, ran 16 agents simultaneously, 2,000 cloud code sessions, $20,000 in API costs. The result, a 100,000 lines of Rust that compile into a C compiler. that C compiler builds the Linux kernel on x86 ARM and risk 5 passes 99% of GCC torture tests the setup is one line in your settings JSON enable the experimental flag choose your display mode T-mark split panes for visual monitoring or in process with shift down to cycle between teammates use delegate mode with shift tab so the lead only coordinates and never starts coding itself with three to five teammates and five to six tasks each wait 91 1% of solo AI builders quit within 3 months. === JSON Render: The Pattern That Solves AI's Biggest Problem! ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1foEhU6dcLA

526% growth, 14 packages, seven rendering platforms, one JSON spec that replaces AI generated code entirely. This is JSON render and Versal just bet against the entire industry, [music] 526%. That is how much JSON [music] renders npm downloads grew in a single month from 31,000 to almost 200,000. [music] But those developers are not downloading just another component library. They are running from a real problem. Every time AI generates raw code, you are one hallucinated, dangerously set inner HTML away from shipping an XSS vector to production. There are now five competing approaches to AI [music] generated interfaces and Jason Render made the most controversial bet of all. But what if the AI never generated code at all? That is the core insight behind what I call the catalog pattern. Instead of letting AI write react, you let AI write data structured, validated, typed JSON. Then you render it yourself. The pipeline is three steps. Define a catalog of your components using sort schemas. The AI produces a JSON spec constraint to that catalog. Then render it on React, View, Mobile, PDF, email, image, or video. Seven platforms, one spec, zero hallucinated components. The catalog is where the catalog pattern gets brilliant. You call define catalog. Pass your sod schemas and list every component the AI is allowed to use. Props, children, actions, all type safe. Here is the double duty trick. The catalog constraints what the renderer accepts and it generates the system prompt for the L LM. One call to catalog.prompt prompt gives the model optimized instructions listing every component it can produce. Think of it like a restaurant menu. The AI is the chef. It can cook anything on the menu but nothing off it. Validation catches the rest. Most generative UI makes you wait. The model finishes. The response passes. Then your dashboard appears all at once. JSON render does not work that way. The AI streams JSON L one JSON patch per line following RFC6902. Each patch adds or updates a component in the live spec. The use I stream hook renders each component the instant it arrives. No loading spinners. Your dashboard builds itself in real time, piece by piece, as the model thinks. You're probably thinking realtime streaming is impressive, but it is still just rendering components. And that was just one package. There are 13 more. JSON render ships 14 packages total. Then four state management adapters, Zustand, Redux, Jotai, Xstate. plus a codition package that exports AI generated dashboards as standalone React projects with zero runtime dependency. Connecting AI to this entire system takes seven lines of code. Server side pass catalog.prompt as the system prompt to stream text from the versal AI SDK. Return the stream three lines client side call use I stream with your endpoint drop in a renderer. four lines. Because the catalog generates plain text instructions, not framework bindings, it is completely model agnostic. Claude, GPT, Gemini, Llama swap models without touching a single component. Here's where most developers get honest about which one they're actually at. Level zero, manual. You type the code. AI suggests the next line, a faster tap key. Level one assisted, you write the important parts. AI handles discrete tasks, unit tests, dock strings, modest gains. Level two, paired programming. You hand off the boring work and stay in flow. Most teams top out here. Level four, autonomous execution. You [clears throat] write a spec. You leave. You come back and check if the test pass. You're not really a developer anymore. You're a product manager. Level five, the dark factory. Spec goes in. Working software comes out. No human writes the code. No human reviews it. What does level five look like in production? It builds the software to stop AI from gaming its own tests. They store behavioral specifications outside the codebase. The agent never sees them during development. Same concept as a hold out data set in machine learning. This is the direct answer to the claim that AI will just optimize for passing its tests. Output 16,000 lines of Rust, 9,500 lines of Go, 6,700 of TypeScript production security software. It ships. Why are 90% of developers still at level two? There's a pattern called the J curve. When you bolt AI onto an existing workflow, productivity actually dips before it rises. Sometimes for weeks, sometimes for months. It's like putting a new engine in a car with an old transmission. The gears grind. Most organizations hit the bottom of that curve, interpret it as proof the tools don't work, and revert. They never make it to the upswing. Three blockers keep teams stuck. First, legacy code bases with no test coverage. You can't hand a 10-year-old monolith to an autonomous agent on day one. That's a precondition failure, not a tool failure. Second, teams that misread the dip. Slowing velocity looks like evidence AI doesn't work, not evidence the workflow is in transition. And third, spec. a brutal discovery that your requirements have always been imprecise and AI just makes it visible. The teams that cross the JC curve redesign everything. For everyone, adequate is no longer a viable career position because adequate is what the models do. But there's a deeper framework emerging for what this actually looks like as a practice. It starts with a picture of a bubble. But that's the simple part of the story. Everyone's talking about levels and frameworks. Let's go beyond it. Picture a bubble. The air inside is everything AI agents can do reliably today. The air outside is everything that still requires a person. The surface of that bubble, that thin membrane between the two, is where the interesting work is happening right now. As AI improves, the bubble inflates. Tasks that set on the surface migrate inside. But notice what happens when a bubble expands. The surface area grows too. More boundary to operate at. More places for human judgment, not less. The frontier doesn't shrink, it expands. That surface has a name, frontier operations. The first workforce skill in history that expires on a roughly quarterly cycle and renews every time the bubble grows. Five skills that persist across the expanding surface. Boundary sensing. Maintaining accurate intuition about where the human agent boundary sits today, not where it sat 3 months ago. Opus 4.5 couldn't retrieve from deep and long documents. 3 months later, Opus 4.6 scores 93%. The skill is maintaining calibration, not having it once. Seam design, structuring handoffs between human and agent so they're clean and recoverable. Which phases of a project are fully agent executable? Which ones need you in the loop? Failure model maintenance. Knowing how agents fail at the current edge. Not just that they fail, but which task type produces which failure mode. Capability forecasting. Reading the swells, not predicting the wave. Position where the next capability jump will land. Like a surfer who reads the ocean, not a fortune teller who guesses the weather. Leverage calibration. Deciding where your attention creates the most value. When you're overseeing dozens of agent streams at once, you cannot review everything at the same depth. The skill is triaging your own attention in real time. These are not a checklist. They are simultaneous, integrated, and continuous. A practice like driving involves steering, speed, route awareness, and hazard perception all at the same time. These five operations are what separate the two worlds we started with. the people on the surface of the bubble from the people standing inside it. And here's why this can't be automated away. When a task migrates inside the bubble, the surface expands outward. The person who operates at the frontier moves with it. Here's the insight that ties both frameworks together. For decades, the bottleneck was implementation speed. That's what the entire industry optimized for. Headcount, tooling, sprints, story points. That bottleneck has moved. It's spec quality. Now, how precisely can you describe what you want to a machine that has no judgment and cannot ask a follow-up question. A bad prompt says, "Build me a login page." A good spec says, "Build a login endpoint. Return a JWT with 24-hour expiry. Reject bad credentials with a non-specific 401 to prevent user enumeration. and log every attempt to the audit table. Same goal, completely different outcome. The dark factory doesn't reduce the demand for deep understanding. It makes it an absolute law. You now have two frameworks. Six levels tell you where you are. Five frontier operations tell you what to do about it. Go figure out which level you're actually on and be honest about your boundary sense. === Intent Engineering vs Context Engineering | Which Actually Works? ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=jia2IcIuhCM

$60 million saved, every metric green. But what if the AI was optimizing for the wrong thing the entire time? This is intent engineering, and it changes everything. Here's a story that should terrify every AI team. Cler deployed an AI agent for customer service. In its first month, it handled 2.3 million conversations. Resolution time dropped from 11 minutes to just two minutes. It replaced over 700 human agents and saved the company $60 million. Every metric was green. Every dashboard looked amazing. But here's what the dashboards didn't show. Customer satisfaction started to slip. Nuanced problems got generic responses. The AI was so efficient at closing tickets that it optimized speed over actually helping people. And Cler eventually acknowledged they needed to bring humans back into the loop. They optimized perfectly for the wrong thing. And that's the most dangerous kind of failure, the one that looks like success until it's too late. This isn't just a Cler problem. MIT found that 95% of generative AI pilots fail to deliver measurable impact. Gutner predicts 40% of Agentic AI projects will be cancelled by 2027. And companies are still pouring 20 to 50% of their digital transformation budgets into AI automation. We're building AI systems that work brilliantly at doing the wrong things. the root cause. We've gotten great at telling AI what to do and giving it the right information, but we never told it what success actually looks like. Think of it as three layers of engineering AI systems. Layer one is prompt engineering, the art of crafting the right words. What should I say to get a good response? Layer two is context engineering. And by 2026, this is a full discipline in its own right. Andre Garbati called it the delicate art and science of filling the context window with just the right information. Context engineers, now often called context architects, don't just dump data into rack pipelines. They map information flows, curate knowledge, and structure exactly what the model sees at runtime. Their question, what does the model need to know? Now, here's a distinction that matters. Context engineering and intent engineering are not the same thing. They're complimentary. Context engineering solves the feeding problem. It controls what information the model has access to. But even with perfect context, Cler proves you can still optimize for the completely wrong thing. That's the gap intent engineering fills. It asks what must be accomplished. What does success actually look like? Each layer builds on the last. Prompts live inside context. Context lives inside intent. Skip that outer layer and you get cloner. Here's the best definition I've found. Intent engineering is the discipline of making goals, values, trade-offs, and decision boundaries machine readable, and machine actionable. Let me break that down. A prompt tells AI what to generate. Context tells AI what to know. Intent tells AI what to want. Instead of saying generate a financial summary, intent engineering says enable leadership to make a funding decision within five minutes by presenting the three most critical financial indicators. Instead of handle customer tickets fast, it says resolve tier one issues while keeping satisfaction above 4.2 and escalating any frustration signals immediately. That second approach, it's the difference between an AI that hits its numbers and one that actually solves the problem. So what does intent engineering actually look like in practice? There's a seven component framework that makes this concrete. First, the objective. What problem are you solving and why does it matter? Second, desired outcomes. What observable changes should happen from the user's perspective? Third, health metrics. And this is the game changer. What metrics must not degrade while the agent pursues its goal? This is what Cler missed. They optimized resolution time without protecting customer satisfaction. Fourth, strategic context, the operating environment the agent needs to understand. Fifth, constraints split into soft constraints that guide behavior and hard constraints enforced in code. Sixth, decision autonomy. Which decisions can the agent make alone versus which require human approval? And seventh, stop rules. When should the agent hold, escalate, or mark a task as complete? Define all seven. And your agent knows not just what to do, but what to protect while doing it. Wait, 91% of solo AI builders quit within three months. But not this group. Over a thousand builders, daily hangouts where you debug together. Weekly workshops that actually level you up. A full aentic coding course. 72 lessons, 12 modules. From zero to shipping multi- aent systems, people share projects, brainstorm ideas, help each other in real time. This is where AI builders actually make it. Link in description, 10% off. Join us. Back to the video. Now, let's see this in action. Without intent engineering, a customer support agent gets one instruction. Handle customer tickets. The result? It optimizes for speed. Closes tickets fast. Generic answers everywhere. Sound familiar? Now, with intent engineering, the objective is helping customers resolve issues without frustration. Desired outcomes include the customer confirming their issue is resolved and no repeat tickets within 24 hours. Health metrics keep satisfaction above 4.2 and escalation quality stable. Constraints say never offer refunds over $50 without human approval. And stop rules say if a customer expresses frustration, escalate to a human immediately. Same agent, same AI model. Completely different behavior. The technology didn't change. The intent did. Now, here's the fair question. Isn't intent engineering just good system design with a fancy name? Partially, yes. But here's why that argument falls apart. If everyone already knew how to do this. 95% of AI pilots wouldn't fail. If it were obvious, companies wouldn't keep optimizing AI for the wrong metrics while real outcomes suffer. Having a formal framework makes this teachable, repeatable, and auditable. The distinction between health metrics and hard guardrails is genuinely new. and genuinely useful. Here's the other question I hear. Isn't this just context engineering? No. And the distinction matters. Context engineering feeds the model. Intent engineering directs it. You can architect a perfect information flow and still build the next cler. If you never defined what winning looks like and most importantly, intent engineering makes the implicit explicit. Plenty of organizations think they have intent defined. What they actually have are instructions and those are not the same thing. Here's the thing that might surprise you. If you've taken the agentic coding course, you've already been doing intent engineering. The PIV loop plan, implement, validate maps directly to this framework. The plan phase defines objectives, acceptance criteria, and validation criteria up front. That's intent capture. The implement phase executes within defined constraints and boundaries. That's intent execution. And the validate phase measures outcomes against those criteria. That's intent verification. Every task in the PIV loop has explicit success conditions. That's literally what stop rules are. So intent engineering isn't some new thing you need to learn from scratch. It's the formalization of what structured agentic workflows have been doing all along. So here's your action plan. Start with one agent. just one. Define its objective. What problem is it solving? Define its desired outcomes. What should change when it succeeds? Define its health metrics. What must not get worse? And define its stop rules. When should it halt? That's it. Four definitions. Don't let your AI optimize for the wrong thing. Don't be the next cautionary tale. If this breakdown was useful, hit subscribe for more deep dives into Agentic AI and practical frameworks. Check out the Agentic coding course linked in the description. It's where the PIV loop and intent engineering connect directly to code. And drop a comment telling me, are you already doing intent engineering without knowing it?

=== This Open-Source AI Coding Agent Crushes Claude Code ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=yAx8-_IYdWI

31,000 GitHub stars, 30 plus LLM providers, $0 block, open source, the AI agent that 10,000 engineers use daily. Recipes, sub aents, security, red teaming. This changes everything. You're paying $200 a month for an AI coding assistant. It's powerful. It writes great code, but you can't switch models. You can't run it offline. You can't share workflows with your team. And if pricing changes, you're locked in. But what if I told you the company that built Square, Cash App, and Title, a company with 10,000 engineers, built their own AI agent internally, watched it save 50 to 75% of development time, and then just open sourced it. It's called Goose. It works with any LLM. It runs locally. It's completely free. And in December 2025, Amazon, Google, Microsoft, and Anthropic all agreed to co-govern it under the Linux Foundation. This isn't a weekend side project. This is infrastructure. Let's start with what makes goose genuinely different. Every AI coding assistant today forces a trade-off. Clot code locks you into clot models. Cursor curates their model selection. GitHub copilot runs on OpenAI's ecosystem. Goose is the only tool that hits all four simultaneously. Highly agentic. It doesn't suggest code. It installs, executes, edits, and tests autonomously. model agnostic 30 plus providers switch midsession fully local run it offline with Olma zero data leaves your machine and free Apache 2.0 zero, no subscriptions, no rate limits that reset every 5 hours. Block didn't build Goose as a product. They built it because no commercial tool satisfied all four constraints for their engineering teams. Then they gave it away. Under the hood, Goose runs a six-step agent loop. A human request comes in. It gets sent to the LLM provider along with all available tools. The model returns tool calls as JSON. Goose executes them and sends results back. After each cycle, a context revision step removes outdated information to save tokens. Then the model delivers its response and the loop restarts. Here's the clever part. When errors happen, invalid JSON, missing tools, failed execution, goose doesn't crash. It captures the error and sends it back to the model as a tool response. The model self-corrects. No stack traces in your terminal. No manual restarts. The agent heals itself. The core is written in Rust for performance with a TypeScript electron desktop app for the GUI. 58% Rust, 33% TypeScript. Fast where it matters familiar where you interact. Goose's extension system is the model context protocol. Every extension is an MCP server exposing three primitives, tools for executable functions, resources for data access, and prompts for task templates. 3,000 plus MCP servers are available today. GitHub, Slack, Docker, Playright, PostgresQL, Stripe, Snowflake. The list is massive and growing daily. But here's what separates this from plug-in ecosystems you've seen before. Extensions built for goose work in cloud desktop. They work in cursor. They work in client. MCP is a universal protocol and goose was designed around it from day one. Building your own takes minutes with fast MCP and Python. Configure it in your config.ml. Distribute Fire Pipi and share oneclick install links. The ecosystem isn't just large, it's interoperable. === 💡Browser-Native Linux — Engineering Genius or Insanity? ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=xsFL8dbD4Vo

Linux in your browser. No VM, no container, no server. Someone literally mapped web APIs to Linux CISLS. The internet is divided. Is this genius engineering or complete insanity? Let's find out. Someone just turned your Chrome tab into a full Linux terminal. Not a simulation, not a toy. An actual bash shell with 60 plus Unix commands running natively in your browser. And the internet has opinions. This is lifeo.sha. SH and its core idea is wild. Instead of emulating Linux inside the browser, it flips the script entirely. The browser is the kernel. Index DB becomes your file system. The fetch API handles networking. Web workers become your processes. It maps browser APIs directly to PH6 style cyslls. So what can you actually do? You get ls, cat, grap, orc, set, the full Unix toolkit. Pipe commands together. Redirect output. Tap completion works. Your files persist across page refreshes thanks to index DB. You can even run node scripts directly. It feels shockingly close to a real terminal. Under the hood, there's a clean layer stack. At the bottom, browser API wrappers form the kernel. Above that, a virtual file system with a full iode tree. Then a complete bash shell with Lexa, parser, and interpreter. And on top, 60 plus command implementations. But here's the catch. It only runs JavaScript. No compiled binaries, no true process isolation, and browser storage quotas still apply. But Liveo O isn't the only game in town. WebVM uses actual x86 emulation through web assembly. It can run real Linux binaries, but takes seconds to boot and is much heavier. Versail's just bash simulates a shell specifically for AI agents. And then there's life o instant boot, tiny footprint, but JavaScript only. Different tools for genuinely different jobs. So the real question is, is this actually useful? AI agent sandboxing, maybe. Learning Linux basics, definitely promising. Interactive product demos could be a gamecher, or is it just a really impressive weekend project? Honestly, I'm not sure yet. That's where you come in. Would you actually use this? Is browser as kernel genius or madness? Drop your take in the comments. I genuinely want to know what you think. And if you want more deep dives like this, hit subscribe. Let's figure this out together.

No. Let's start with the foundation. Clot.m MD is a markdown file you add to your project. Clot reads it at the start of every session. Always. No exceptions. Think of it as the company handbook. A project level file that your whole team shares via version control. And a local file just for you for this specific project. More specific always wins. A skill is a markdown file called skill.md that teaches Claude how to do something specific. It has a description that tells Claude when to use it and instructions that Claude follows when it activates. Here's the magic. You don't have to type a SL command. You say, "Deploy to production," and your deployment checklist activates. Only the description is in context at all times. The full skill content loads on demand. Your PR review checklist isn't consuming tokens while you're debugging. It loads when you actually ask for a review. Skills can also be SL commands. Personal skills in your home directory follow you everywhere. Project skills include/skills get shared with your team through version control. Claude.md loads into every conversation. Always consuming tokens. Always in context. Skills load on demand only when relevant. Minimal context cost. Here's the decision framework. They're specialized AI assistants that run in their own context window, completely isolated from your main conversation. When you ask Claude to research a codebase, it can delegate to an explore sub agent. That agent reads dozens of files, searches through directories, analyzes the architecture. All of that verbose output stays in the sub aents context. Only the relevant summary comes back to your main conversation. Your context window stays clean. A code reviewer that remembers patterns it's seen across sessions. A debugger that builds up knowledge about common issues in your codebase. The key rule. if it should run in isolation with its own context. Use a sub agent. Hooks are fundamentally different from everything we've covered so far. Skills add knowledge. Sub agents delegate tasks, but hooks hooks fire on events automatically, deterministically. No hoping the LLM makes the right choice. A hook is a shell command that executes at a specific point in clot code's life cycle. Pre-tool use fires before clot runs a tool. Post tool use fires after. session, start fires when you begin, stop fires when Claude finishes responding. There are 15 event types in total. Here's a concrete example. You write a pre-tool use hook that matches the bash tool. Every time Claude tries to run a shell command, your script checks the command first. If it contains RM-rf, exit code two, blocked, destructive, command denied. Claude never runs it. Another example, post tool use hook matching write and edit. Every time Claude saves a file, your hook runs prettier to auto format. Every file, every time. Consistent formatting without even thinking about it. The critical distinction. Skills are request driven. They activate when you asked for something. Hooks are event driven. They fire on every matching event regardless of what you asked for. You don't hope Claude remembers to lint. The hook guarantees it. Hooks can even use an LLM to make decisions. A prompt-based hook sends context to a fast model that returns a yes or no decision. An agent-based hook spawns a sub agent that can read files and check conditions before deciding. Hooks aren't limited to simple shell scripts. The key rule, if it should happen automatically on every matching event, use a hook. The last piece, MCP stands for model context protocol. It's an open source standard for connecting AI tools to external services. Need claude to check your sentry errors. Connect the sentry MCP server. Want to query your Postgress database directly? Add the database MCP server. GitHub pull requests, Jira issues, Figma designs, Slack messages. There are over 100 official servers in the registry. You connect them with a single command. Claude MCP add. Choose a transport type and you're done. Claude gets access to new tools from external services without any custom code. MCP tools appear alongside Claude's built-in tools. You can use them in hooks, in skills, in sub agents. The whole system composes together. The key rule, if Claude needs external tools or data, connect an MCP server. Let's bring it all together. Five features, five questions, one answer each. Should Claude always notice cloud.md. Should claude notice sometimes skills. Should this run in isolation, sub aents? Should this happen automatically on events hooks? Does clot need external tools? MCP servers. Now look at context window impact. Clot.m MD is high. It's loaded every session consuming tokens where they're relevant or not. Skills are low. Only the description sits in context until invoked. Sub aents and hooks are zero. Sub aents run in their own context window. Hooks run outside Claude's context entirely. MCP is moderate, but tool search dynamically loads tools on demand to mitigate it. Your context window is precious. Use it wisely. A real world setup uses multiple features simultaneously. Here's what a well-configured project looks like. Claw.mde handles your always on standards. Use PNPM. TypeScript strict mode. Never mutate the database directly. Skills handle task specific expertise. A PR review skill with your team's checklist. A deployment skill with your step-by-step procedure. A commit skill with your message format. Sub agents handle delegation. A code reviewer with read only access. A researcher that explores documentation without cluttering your context. Hooks handle automation. Auto format every file cloud touches. Block destructive shell commands. Run tests after every edit. MCP connects external services. GitHub for pull requests. Sentry for error monitoring. Postgress for data queries. Each handles its own specialty. Don't force everything into one feature. You can use all five at the same time. They compose. They complement. They don't compete. If you find yourself explaining the same thing to Claude repeatedly, that's a skill waiting to be written. Here's how to start. Open your project run/init to bootstrap a claude.md. That's your foundation. Then the next time you repeat yourself, write a skill. The next time you want guardrails, add a hook. === RAG Doesn't Work for Code — Here's Why ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=y7LvDD7S2TU

Rag is dead. Or is it? Clot code removed it. Ada never used it. But kill it for your documents and you'll pay for it. Here's exactly when to ditch rag and when it saves you. Rack is dead. It's all over LinkedIn. All over X. And honestly, the best AI coding tools in the world seem to agree. Claude Code, Cursor, Ader, Klein. These are the tools serious developers trust right now. And one by one, they've been quietly walking away from traditional rack. Some of them didn't just stop recommending it. They tried it, built it, shipped it, and then ripped it out. But before you delete your vector database and cancel your Pine Cone subscription, stop. Because if you're building a customer support bot, a legal compliance agent, or any system that searches thousands of documents, rag isn't just alive. It's the only thing standing between your agent and complete hallucination. The rag is dead narrative is only half the story. And the half everyone's missing is going to cost teams real money. Let's be precise about what's actually dying. When people say rack is dead, they mean one very specific pipeline. You take your documents, you chunk them up. You embed those chunks into a vector database and then you retrieve the most semantically similar chunks when a user asks a question. That specific pattern, traditional rag semantic similarity search, that is what's dying for code. Nick Par, the creator of cluin published a piece calling this the mind virus of AI development. The default assumption that every agent needs a vector database injected into teams who never stop to ask why. Think about the world a coding agent lives in. First, perfect spelling. Every function name, every class, every variable exact. If get user by ID has a typo, your code is broken. There's no ambiguity to search around. Second, built-in organization. The file structure is the index. Your agent already knows that authentication logic lives in the service of directory. That's not a retrieval problem. That's navigation. Third, terminal superpowers. RIP grap glob zed. These tools find exact patterns across an entire codebase in milliseconds. No embedding, no chunking, no latency, just results. Code is structured data and perfectly structured data doesn't need semantic search. Don't take my word for it. Boris Journey is one of the core maintainers of Cloud Code. When someone asked why Cloud Code doesn't use embedding models or traditional rag, his answer was direct. Early versions of Cloud Code actually did use rack with a local vector database. They built it. They shipped it. And then they found that Agentic Search, just giving the model terminal tools to navigate the codebase directly, worked better. So they removed the vector database entirely. The world's most used AI coding tool, decided rag was dead for code, and they had the data to prove it. ADA took a different approach entirely. No vector database, no embeddings at all. Instead, they built something called a repo map using a tool called Treitter. ADA passes your entire codebase and generates a compact structural index, file names, class definitions, function signatures, call relationships. That index goes straight into the model system prompt. The agent sees the architecture of your code before it reads a single line. No retrieval pipeline to maintain, no index going still, just structured context exactly where the model needs it. Nick P didn't just build Klein. He's one of the original creators of Klein, the tool that became it. And his take is blunt. The belief that coding agents need rag is a mind virus. A compelling idea that spreads through teams without anyone questioning whether it actually applies to their use case. You watch teams spend weeks engineering retrieval pipelines for their coding agents only to get worse results than a simple grab command. If you're building a coding agent and you're reaching for a vector database, ask yourself why. The answer might be that you just assumed you needed it. Now, here's where it gets interesting. Because everything I just said, it only applies to code. The moment you leave the perfectly structured world of software and enter the world of unstructured documents, rag becomes irreplaceable. Here's a simple example. Imagine you're searching 10 million documents for anything related to Star Wars spaceships. Keyboard search finds documents that literally say Star Wars spaceship, but it misses every document that mentions the X-wing or the TIE fighter or the Millennium Falcon. Those are conceptually related, not keyword matches. That gap is exactly what semantic search and vector embeddings solve. Those customer support transcripts, legal filings, internal wikis, medical records, anywhere you have massive collections of natural language. Synonyms matter. Context matters matters. For those use cases, rag isn't dead. It's the only tool that works at scale. And there's a financial argument too. For large knowledge bases, we're talking millions of documents. Traditional RAG is roughly 100 times cheaper than agentic document reading. When you chunk your documents and store embeddings, each query retrieves a small targeted slice of information. Fast, cheap, precise. Without RAG, you're asking your agent to read entire documents every single time. At scale, that's not a minor inefficiency. That's the difference between a sustainable product and one that burns your budget. The chunking pipeline that feels like overhead, that is the efficiency. Here's what the smartest teams are building toward. Instead of committing to one retrieval strategy, you give your agent the ability to choose. Is this an exact identifier lookup? Use RIP Grip. That's a code question. Is this a conceptual search across thousands of documents, wrote to the vector database? That's a document question. The agent reads the query, understands the data type, and picks the right tool. Llama index calls this agentic retrieval. It's not a replacement for rack. It's rack made smarter. And as models get better at reasoning about their own search strategies, this hybrid approach is going to become the standard. So, is rack dead for autonomous coding agents? Yes. Code is structured. Grab beats embeddings. Every major coding tool has figured this out. For everything else, documents, knowledge bases, any system built on natural language at scale, absolutely not. Rag is not dead. RG is essential. The answer depends entirely on your data. If you found this useful, subscribe. I'm covering more on advanced rag patterns and aentic search strategies, including how to build the hybrid systems that pick the right approach automatically. The tools are getting smarter. So should your retrieval strategy.

=== Your Local Dev Setup Is Outdated (Here's Why) ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rV-tK6i6jnc

Five services running. Which port is which? Named local host URLs. [music] AI agents that actually work. Monora routting in two commands. Here's everything portless does [music] and why you need it. Right now, you probably have three or four services running locally. Maybe five. [music] Front end API server admin panel off service web hook proxy. Then it happens. That >> [music] >> error address already in use. Which process is hawking the port? Kill it. Retry. By the time you fix it, you've [music] completely lost your train of thought. Every developer has been here more than once. [music] But here's what nobody actually talks about. Your AI coding assistant has the same problem. When cursor or cloud code writes a fetch call, it guesses the port. [music] And half the time it's wrong. The API is on 3001. The agent hardcoded 3000. [music] Test fails. Config breaks. You think the AI is being dumb. Really, your local environment gave it nothing to work with. This isn't an AI problem. It's an environment problem. Your production app api.mmyapp.com. Clear, readable, memorable. Your local app, localhost col 3001. A meaningless number. Port numbers made sense in the '90s. You ran one server, one port. Now you run five services simultaneously. And we're still using digits. The fix isn't a smarter AI. It's a smarter environment. That fix is portless, an open- source tool from Versal Labs. One command. You wrap your normal dev command with portless. Give your app a name and it appears at a named.lohost URL. My app.lohost port 1355. Instead of five port numbers, you'll forget by morning, you get five names you'll never forget. web.mmyapp.lohost api.mmyapp.lohost admin.app.lohost or do myapp.lohost hooks.mmyapp.lohost. all running simultaneously, all routed automatically. No config files, no port conflicts ever. The magic is in how portless handles ports behind the scenes. When you run a service through portless, it automatically assigns a random port in the 4,000 range. You never know what that port is, and you don't need to. The proxy maps your name to that port every time. Same URL every session. And if you ever need to bypass portless for any reason, set portless to zero. Zero lock. For monor repos, this is a gamecher. Imagine your stack, a next.js front end, an express API, an admin panel, an o service with regular ports. You need to remember and document four different numbers. Change one update your core config, your oath redirect urist, your entire team's documentation. With portless, each service gets a subdomain. Web.mmyapp for the front end. API.mmyapp for the back end. Admin.mmyapp for the admin panel. One base URL pattern forever stable. Now back to that AI agent problem. When your agent knows your app lives at api.mmyapp.lohost, it writes the correct URL every time. No guessing, no wrong ports. Portless even ships agents.md file. Explicit instructions for AI coding assistants. It's one of the only developer tools built with AI agents as first class users from day one. Stable URLs mean reliable agents. It's that simple. There's one more problem ports create that nobody thinks about until it bites them. Cookies. Set a cookie on local host 3000. It can bleed across to 3001. So does that off tokens session data colliding between your services. With portless, each service is a real subdomain. Cookies are fully isolated. No more mysterious Obugs at 2 in the morning. Portless is from Versal Labs, the experimental team behind Nex.js. It's open- source Apache 2.0 licensed, free forever, and it works with every major framework. Next.js, feed, express, remix, nux, anything that respects the port environment variable, which is basically everything. To get started, npm install-g portless, then portless myapp next. That's it. named URLs immediately. The source is on GitHub adversal labs/portless Apache licensed open-source pull request. Welcome. Your production environment has readable URLs. Your staging environment has readable URLs. There's no good reason your local environment should still run on a number you can't remember. Make it readable today.

=== Why Every AI Developer Needs to Know About WebMCP Now ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=OMu1ykcbNL8

Google just shipped something that changes how AI agents interact with every website on the internet. Here's what WebM MCP means for you. AI agents have been trying to use websites the same way a blindfolded person uses a vending machine. Screenshots, DOM scraping, pixel by pixel guessing, 70% accuracy on a good day. But Google and Microsoft just shipped a W3C standard that turns every website into a structured AI tool. 98% accuracy, 89% fewer tokens. Right now, when Claude or Chat GPT needs to interact with a website, it either takes a screenshot and runs it through a vision model, burning thousands of tokens, or it scrapes the DOM and tries to guess which button does what. No standard protocol, no structured data. Every agent framework reinvents browser automation from scratch. And authentication, forget about it. Separate API keys, oath flows just to click a button. Web MCP Web Model Context Protocol, a W3C community group standard co-authored by Google and Microsoft. It adds a new browser native JavaScript API navigator.mmodel context that lets any website expose structured callable tools directly to AI agents. Think of it as MCP for the front end. Entropic built MCP for backend servers. Google just built it for the browser. The architecture has three components. First, the web page. It registers tools. could be a travel site exposing a flight search function or a code review tool exposing test run status. Second, the browser itself acts as mediator. Chrome sits in the middle enforcing permissions, managing consent, mediating every tool call. Third, the AI agent Claude ChatgPT Gemini discovers available tools and invokes them with structured JSON input. The browser is the trust layer. Web MCP gives you two ways to expose tools. The declarative approach is beautifully simple. Take any existing HTML form, add a few attributes, tool name, tool description, tool autosubmit, and you're done. The browser automatically passes your input fields, and generates a JSON schema. Zero JavaScript required. Your existing forms become AI agent callable tools with five lines of HTML attributes. For more control, the imperative API lets you register tools via JavaScript. Call navigator.mmodel context.provide context with a tools array. Each tool gets a name, description, input schema, and an execute call back. The callback runs in your page's JavaScript context, meaning it shares the user's cookies, session authentication. No separate API keys needed. The agent just calls your function. The performance numbers speak for themselves. Compared to traditional screenshot based agents, 89% improvement in token efficiency, 67% reduction in computational overhead. And task accuracy jumps from roughly 70% to 98%. That's not incremental. That's a paradigm shift in how agents interact with the web. Now, here's the important distinction. This is not Antropics MCP. They're complimentary. MCP is the backend protocol JSON RPC over standard IO connecting AI platforms to serverside tools. Web MCP is the front-end protocol, a browser native API connecting agents to clientside web pages. Together, they are the full stack of AI tool integration. Backend and front end, server and browser. The use cases are everywhere. E-commerce agents that search, filter, and check out through structured tools. Code review boards that check test results and suggest edits. Design tools where you collaborate with AI through natural language task managers that let agents add, complete, and organize without touching the DOM. Currently available in Chrome 146 canary behind a flag. The spec is a W3C community group draft, not a formal standard yet. Security sections are still placeholder. Prompt injection through tool descriptions, data exfiltration, through tool chaining, real concerns that need real answers. But the human in the loop API with request user interaction is a solid start. Web MCP is the missing bridge between AI agents and the web. It's not production ready yet. But if you're building agentpowered experiences, start learning it now. Drop your questions in the comments. Subscribe for more deep dives into the AI developer stack. I'll see you in the next one.

=== Claude Code's Agent Teams Built a $20K Compiler in Hours ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=PUnbKtxobs0

In this video, four agents collaborating in real time, one $20,000 C compiler, and a pattern that makes agent teams actually reliable. Four plot code instances running at the exact same time on the same codebase. But here's what makes this different from anything you've seen before. They're actually talking to each other, coordinating tasks, sharing discoveries, and Enthropic used 16 of them to build an entire C compiler for $20,000. This isn't sub agents. This is something new. So what exactly are agent teams? Think of it like spinning up an entire dev team except every member is claude. You have a team lead. That's your main claude code session. It creates the team, spawns teammates, and coordinates all the work. Each teammate is a fully independent claude code instance with its own context window. But the magic is in the connective tissue. There's a shared task list that everyone can see and claim work from. And there's a mailbox, a messaging system where agents send updates, share findings, and coordinate directly with each other. Let me break down the architecture. At the center is your team lead. It's the orchestrator. It decides what work needs to happen and who does it. When the lead spawns a teammate, it launches a completely separate cloud code session. Each teammate loads the same project context. Your cloud.md file, your MCP servers, your skills, but it gets its own context window. It does not inherit the conversation history. The shared task list lives locally and it coordinates everything. Tasks have three states, pending, in progress, and completed. They can also have dependencies. So, a task won't unblock until its prerequisite finishes and the messaging system delivers updates automatically. No polling required. Now, here's the question everyone asks. How is this different from sub agents? And the answer matters because it changes when you should use each one. Sub agents are context isolators. You spin one up, it does focused work and sends back a summary. That's it. No coordination, no pure communication, just a result. Agent teams are the opposite. Teammates talk to each other. The back end agent can tell the front- end agent, "Hey, I changed this API endpoint. Update your component." That kind of real-time coordination was impossible with sub agents. They'd step on each other's toes and never know it. But here's the trade-off. Agent teams use two to four times more tokens. Each teammate is a full claw instance. So, the rule of thumb, sub agents for research, agent teams for implementation. Getting started is straightforward. Agent teams are experimental. You have to opt in. Either set the environment variable claw code experimental agent teams to one or add it to your settings.json. For the split pane mode where you can see all terminals at once, you need timox or item 2. On Windows, you'll need WSL. There are two display modes in process where everything runs in your main terminal and you use shift updown to navigate or split panes where each teammate gets its own visible terminal. The communication system is where agent teams really shine. There are three message types. Direct messages go to one specific teammate. Broadcasts go to everyone, but use these sparingly because cost scales with team size and shutdown requests ask a teammate to gracefully exit. When the lead spawns a teammate, it passes a prompt with context about the role. The teammate picks that up and starts working. As tasks complete, the shared task list updates automatically. Block tasks unblock when their dependencies resolve. And file locking prevents race conditions when multiple teammates try to claim the same task. Here's where it gets powerful. Imagine you want to review a pull request. Instead of one agent doing everything, you spawn three reviewers, one focused on security, one on performance, one on test coverage. They work simultaneously, each applying a different lens to the same code. But because they can communicate, the security reviewer might flag something that the test coverage reviewer needs to address. The performance reviewer spots an optimization that changes the security picture. The synthesize everything into a comprehensive report. A single agent gravitates toward one type of issue. A team covers all the angles. Implementation is where agent teams destroy sub agents. Picture a full stack feature. Database, back end, front end. With sub agents, the database agent defines a schema. The backend agent builds an API on assumptions and the front end guesses at the contract. Everything breaks. With agent teams, the database agent defines the schema and messages the backend agent directly. Here's the contract. The back end builds against the real schema and tells the front end, here's the actual API. Realtime coordination means fewer rewrites, fewer bugs, and way less wasted work. This is the collaboration that was missing. But even with communication, there is a patent that makes everything more reliable. I call it contract first spawning. Instead of launching all agents in parallel and hoping they coordinate, you sequence the critical handoffs. The lead analyzes your plan and identifies the contract chain. What depends on what? It spawns the most upstream agent first. For a fullstack feature, that's the database agent. The database agent builds the schema, then sends the contract back to the lead. Only then does the lead spawn the backend agent with that schema as context. The back end finishes its contract and the front-end agent spawns last. You still get parallel work because each agent keeps building after sending its contract. But the foundation is solid. Four practices that separate reliable teams from chaos. First, give teammates enough context. They don't inherit the lits conversation. Include task specific details in the spawn prompt. Second, size tasks appropriately. Too small and coordination overhead kills you. Too large and teammates work too long without check-ins. Aim for self-contained units with clear deliverables. Third, avoid file conflicts. Two teammates editing the same file leads to overrides. Break work so each teammate owns different files. And fourth, monitor and steer. Check in on progress, redirect approaches that aren't working, and synthesize findings as they come in. Let's be honest about the limitations. This is experimental. Session resumption doesn't restore in process teammates. Task status can lag. Teammates sometimes forget to mark things complete. Shutdown can be slow. You get one team per session with no nested teams. The lead is fixed. You can't transfer leadership. And split panes don't work in VS Code's integrated terminal or Windows terminal. Token costs are two to four times higher than single sessions. Know these trade-offs before you commit. But here's proof of what's possible. Antropic used 16 agents running together with agent teams to build an entire C compiler from scratch. Hundreds of thousands of lines of code. A task that would cost a human dev team hundreds of thousands of dollars done for 20,000 in API costs. No single agent could have pulled this off. This is what coordinated parallel intelligence looks like at scale. Agent teams are experimental, imperfect, and absolutely the future of agentic development. Enable the feature, install T-Max and try it yourself. Start simple. a code review or a multi-file refactor, then scale up. If this breakdown helped you, hit subscribe for more deep dives into the cutting edge of AI coding.

=== Don't Use Claude Code Like ChatGPT—Use It Like This Instead ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=v8xxBNb7dxQ

How does clot code actually work under the hood? In this video, we'll break down the agentic loop, the five built-in tool categories, and the safety features that keep you in control. Let's go. You type one sentence into your terminal. Seconds later, Claude Code has read 12 files, edited four, and fixed your failing tests, all without you touching a thing. But what actually happened between your prompt and that result? Under the hood, it's an agentic loop, a system of tools, verification, and memory. And once you understand that system, your results get dramatically better. Clot code is an agentic assistant that lives in your terminal, not just suggestions, actual action. It reads your code, edits files, runs commands, and checks its own work. Think of it as a capable colleague sitting in your terminal ready to take on entire tasks from start to finish. Let's see exactly how it works. Everything Clot Code does follows the agentic loop. Three phases running continuously until the job is done. Phase one, gather context. Clot reads files, searches your code base, and builds understanding of the problem. Phase two, take action. It edits code, runs shell commands, installs packages, whatever the task requires. Phase three, verify results. It runs your tests, checks the output, and confirms the fix actually works. Then it loops back. Each step feeds into the next. If the tests fail, it reads the errors, adjusts the code, and tries again. And here's the key. You're always in the loop, too. You can interrupt at any point to steer claude in a different direction. It works autonomously but stays responsive to your input. The agentic loop is powered by two ingredients. Models handle the reasoning. Claude reads your code, understands how components connect, and figures out what needs to change. But reasoning alone isn't enough. Tools provide the action or by reading files, editing code, running commands, searching the web. Without tools, Claude is just a text generator. With tools, it becomes a true agent. Claude comes with five categories of built-in tools. File operations. Read, edit, create, and rename files across your project. Search. Find files by pattern. Search content with rejects. Explore entire code bases. Execution. Run shell commands. Start servers. Execute tests. Use Git Web. Search the web. Fetch documentation. Look up error messages and code intelligence. See type errors after edits. Jump to definitions. Find references across your code. So do here's what makes this powerful. When you say fix the failing tests, Claude chains these tools together automatically. Run the tests, read the errors, find the source files, edit the fix, run the tests again. Six steps. Zero manual intervention. When you run claude in a directory, it gets access to everything you'd need. Your entire project files and subdirectories. Your terminal, any command you can run claude can run too. your get state, the current branch, uncommitted changes, recent history, your claude.mmd file, persistent project instructions that Claude reads every session, and any extensions you configure, MCP servers for external services, skills for workflows, and sub agents for delegated tasks. Every conversation with clot code is a session saved locally. But here's an important detail. Sessions are independent. Each new session starts fresh without history from previous ones. To pick up where you left off, use continue. It resumes the same session appending new messages. Or use fork session to branch off and try a different approach without affecting the original. For parallel work, use git work trees. Each work tree gets its own directory and its own session. And for knowledge that should persist across sessions, put it in claude.md. Claude has two safety mechanisms to keep you in control. First checkpoints. Before CLA edits any file, it snapshots the current contents. If something goes wrong, press escape twice to rewind to a previous state. Every change is reversible. Second, permissions. Press shift plus tab to cycle through four modes. Default mode asks before file edits and shell commands. Auto accept edits trusts file changes but still asks for commands. Plan mode is read only. Claude explores and plans without making changes and delegate mode coordinates work through agent teams. Five tips to get dramatically better results from claude code. One, be specific upfront. Reference files mention constraints. Point to example patterns. Specific prompts often succeed on the first attempt. Two, give Claude something to verify against. Include test cases or define the expected output. Three, explore before implementing. Use plan mode to analyze first, then implement. Four, delegate, don't dictate. Give context and direction, then trust Claude to figure out the details. Five, it's a conversation. If the first attempt isn't right, iterate. You don't have to start over. === Your Claude Code is Broken Without This One Practice ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Uw_i2i7bPJk

In this video, verification loops that transform Claude's output. The four-phase workflow, context management secrets and the antipatterns killing your productivity. You've been using Claude code at maybe 20% of what it can actually do. The official docs have a line that stopped me cold. They call verification the single highest leverage practice for better results. Not better prompts, not bigger context windows. Verification. Here are eight practices that change everything. The number one best practice. Give Clot a way to check its own work. Without verification, Claude produces plausible looking code that breaks on edge cases. With verification, it selfcorrects. Here's the pattern. Don't just say implement email validation. Say write validate email. Here are test cases. User@acample.com returns true. Invalid returns false. user at.com returns false. Run the tests after. For UI work, tell Claude to take a screenshot and compare it to the design. Paste the reference image right in. For bugs, say, "Fix this. Run the test suite and verify it passes. Address root cause. Don't suppress the error. Always close the loop. Test it. Screenshot it. Verify it. The best workflow follows four phases. Explore, plan, code, commit. First, enter plan mode. Let Claude read files and understand the codebase. Ask questions. No changes yet. Second, create a plan. Press Ctrl G to edit the plan in your text editor before proceeding. Third, switch to normal mode. Implement against the plan. Have Claude write tests and run them. Fourth, commit. Let Claude create the commit and PR. When should you skip planning? If you can describe the diff in one sentence, just do it. Planning is for uncertain approaches and multifile changes. The more precise your instructions, the fewer corrections you'll need. Three rules. Scope the task. Don't say add test for food. Say write a test for food.py covering the edge case where the user is locked out. Avoid mocks. Reference existing patterns. Look at how hot dog widget is built. Follow that pattern for a calendar widget. Describe symptoms, not problems. Users report login fails after session timeout. Check the Oflow in source O, especially token refresh. Your claw.md file is your most powerful configuration tool. But most developers get it wrong. Keep it short. For every line, ask would removing this cause Claude to make mistakes. If Claude already does something correctly without the instruction, delete it. What to include? Commands. Claude can't guess. code style that differs from defaults, testing conventions, repo etiquette, environment quirks, what to cut, standard language conventions, detailed API docs, file by file descriptions, anything Claude can infer from reading the code. If Claude keeps ignoring a rule, your file is probably too long. Rules get lost in noise. The best results come from tight feedback loops. Know these shortcuts. Escape stops clawed midaction. Context preserved. Redirect instantly. Double escape opens the rewind menu. Restore code conversation or both. Slash clear resets context between unrelated tasks. Slash compact summarizes and free space. Critical rule. If you've corrected Claude more than twice on the same issue, stop clear. Write a better prompt from scratch. A clean session with a better prompt almost always beats a polluted one. Here's a power move. Use two clawed sessions for quality. The writer reviewer pattern. Session A implements the feature. Session B reviews it with fresh context. The reviewer won't be biased to what code it just wrote. Other patterns. One session writes tests. Another writes code to pass them. One explores. One implements. Git work trees keep each session fully isolated. Different branches, different context, zero interference. Five mistakes killing your results. The kitchen sync session. Mixing unrelated tasks pollutes context. Fix/cle clear between tasks. Correcting over and over after two failures. Start fresh. The bloated claude.md too long. Claude ignores half of it. === Supabase pgvector: The Vector Database Everyone Missed ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=a2b2g5D64d4

In this video, why you probably don't need Pine Cone, the hidden cost of embeddings that nobody talks about, and the simple framework to pick the right solution. You've probably heard about vector databases if you're building anything with AI. But here's the thing, you probably don't need a separate one. Today, I'm showing you why Superbases built-in vector database might be all you need and how to decide between free and paid embeddings. Superbase vector database is powered by something called PG Vector. It's an extension for Postgress that lets you store and search through vector embeddings directly in your database alongside all your other data. Previously, you'd need two separate systems. Your main database for users, orders, and content, plus a dedicated vector database like Pine Cone or BV8 just for AI search. With Superbase, it's all in one place. That means no data synchronization headaches, no managing two different services, no double billing. Your user data, your vectors, your relationships, everything lives in the same Postgress instance you're already running. But what exactly are these vectors? [music] Think of a vector embedding as a mathematical fingerprint for a piece of text. When you send text to an embedding model, it converts that text into a list of numbers, usually between 384 and 1536 of them. These numbers capture the meaning of the text, not just the words. Here's why this matters. If you embed I love pizza and I enjoy pizza, they'll have nearly identical number patterns because they mean the same thing. But I hate pizza will have a different pattern because the meaning changed. This lets you search by meaning, not just keywords. A search for comfortable running shoes can find products labeled cushioned athletic footwear because the vectors are similar. Now you might be thinking, "But I've heard of Pine Cone. Isn't that the standard choice?" Pine Cone is built specifically for vectors. It's optimized for massive scale, billions of vectors. It handles all the infrastructure automatically. Yes, Pine Cone has a free tier, but it's limited to 2 GB of storage and locked to a single region. For anything production ready, you're looking at $50 per month minimum. Meanwhile, Superbase gives you 500 MGB of database storage plus 1 GBTE of file storage for [music] free. And vectors are just part of your existing Postgregql database. No separate service, no sync issues. For 99% of startups and indie projects, superbase vectors are more than enough. Pine cone is for later when you're processing billions of vectors. Most projects never get there. Here's where most tutorials get it wrong. They tell you to use Open AAI to generate your embeddings without explaining what that actually costs. OpenAI's text embedding three small costs 0.02 per million tokens. Sounds cheap, right? Let's do the math. Say you're embedding 10,000 documents. Each document averages 500 tokens. That's 5 million tokens total. Cost about 10 cents. Now scale up. 100,000 documents equals 50 million tokens equals $1. 1 million documents, that's 10 totals. And remember, this isn't a onetime cost. Every time you add or update documents, you pay again. Building a rack system that updates daily, those costs compound fast. But here is what almost nobody talks about. You can generate embeddings completely free using open- source models right inside Superbase Edge functions. Models like GTE small from hugging face are free to use and included in your edge function compute. And here's [music] the thing, the quality difference is tiny. On the standard MTEB benchmark, GTE small scores 61.36, OpenAI's model scores 62.26. That's less than 2% difference that most applications will never notice. Better yet, GTE small uses only 384 dimensions compared to OpenAI's 1536. [music] That means your vectors take up less storage and similarity searches run faster. The trade-off, edge functions take about 100 to 200 milliseconds per embedding instead of OpenAI's instant response. But for batch processing documents, that delay is irrelevant and you're paying $0. Let me show you the actual math for a typical project. Say you're building a documentation search with 100,000 pages. With OpenAI embedded, [music] generating those factors costs about $1. Every time you rebuild your index, maybe weekly, another dollar over a year, you're spending $50 to $100 just on embeddings. Pattern three, error ping pong. Bug appears. Ask AI. New bug. Ask AI again. You never actually learn the highscoring patterns. You stay engaged. Pattern four, code and questions. But if AI stops you from learning, you can't tell good from bad. In critical systems, healthcare, banking, infrastructure, that's not just a skills problem. It's dangerous. So, how do you use AI without hurting your skills? Four simple rules. One, on ask why, not just how. Understand the code. Two, get explanations with code. Make AI teach you. Three, try fixing bugs yourself first. Struggle build skill. Four, code without AI sometimes. Keep your skills sharp on your own. The researchers put it perfectly. AI productivity is not a shortcut to skill. AI is powerful, but it makes good developers better. It doesn't make beginners good.
