=== Claude Subagents are Absolutely Insane ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=nNBw_J1uHho

In this video, we're diving into sub agents, the secret weapon for parallel research and clean context windows. You'll learn why parallelization matters, how context isolation keeps your main thread focused, and when sub agents can actually hurt you. You just asked Claw to research your codebase. 20 files in your context window is stuffed with implementation details you don't need yet. 50 files later, you've lost track of what you were even trying to build. But what if you could offload that entire research task to a parallel universe? Sub agents are preconfigured AI personalities that Claude code can delegate tasks to. Each one gets its own context window completely separate from your main conversation. You configure them with specific tools, a custom [music] system prompt, and they work independently before returning results. Think of them as isolated worker threads for your AI. They do the heavy lifting while your main thread stays clean. Anthropic lists four official benefits. Context preservation, offload heavy tasks to keep your main window light, specialized expertise, focused system prompts for specific jobs, reusability, same sub agent works across projects, and flexible permissions control exactly which tools each agent can access. These are all true, but they're missing the two things that actually matter most. The real power of sub agents is parallelization. You can spawn up to 10 sub aents simultaneously, each tackling a different research angle. Need to understand how to add a new tool to your project? Send five agents out in parallel. One researches the API. One analyzes your codebase structure. One checks integration patterns. One reviews similar implementations. One evaluates token costs. What would take 20 minutes sequentially takes 3 minutes in parallel. That's the game changer. The second superpower is context isolation. Every exploration, every file read, every dead end your sub agent hits, none of that pollutes your main conversation. Your main thread [music] stays surgically focused on implementation. Without sub agents, your context window becomes a junk drawer. With sub agents, research happens in isolation and only the distilled insights come back. But here's what anthropic doesn't emphasize enough. The context handoff problem. There are two handoffs happening. First, your main agent sends instructions to the sub agent. Second, the sub agent sends a summary back. In both steps, detail gets lost. If you ask a sub agent to explore the codebase, you'll get a condensed summary, but you've lost the nuance you'd need to actually implement something. The hand of compression can hurt you. Sub aents are just markdown files in your do.cl/ CL/ agents folder. The front matter defines the configuration name, description, allowed tools, which model to use. Below that goes your system prompt. The description field controls when Claude automatically delegates tasks and the tools array can include MCP servers, letting you create specialized agents for Archon, Playright, or any integration you've configured. Let me show you a common but suboptimal pattern. You tell Claude, "Use the task tool to explore this codebase." A sub agent launches, reads 50 files, and returns a project summary. Sounds useful. But here's the problem. If you now try to implement something, you've lost all the detail that was in those 50 files. Bad use case for sub agents. Now, here's the powerful pattern. You tell Claude, "Send out five parallel sub agents to research." Adding a brave search tool. One agent analyzes architecture patterns. One researches the Brave API. One checks your codebase structure. One reviews paid AI patterns. One evaluates token optimization. All five run simultaneously. Results come back in minutes. Your main thread receives focused findings without ever reading those files directly. That's the right way to use sub agents. This video is part of the Dynamos agentic coding course. We cover everything from the PIV loop methodology to MCP servers, context engineering, and yes, sub agents. If you want to master agentic coding with cloud code, this is your road map. === The workflow that separates functioning AI from chaos ===

In this video, you'll learn the mental model that separates chaotic AI coding from systematic workflows. The PI feed loop. Four letters that change everything about how you work with AI coding assistance. Your AI coding assistant just wrote 500 lines of code. Test pass. Looks beautiful. You ship it. Two hours later, production explodes. Sound familiar? Most developers treat AI coding like a slot machine. Sometimes you win big, sometimes you lose everything. But what if there was a system? Meet the PI feed loop. Plan, implement, validate, iterate. This is the cornerstone mental model for systematic AI coding. Not a framework, not a tool. A way of thinking. The secret? Your own planning and validation. You delegate implementation to AI. Every iteration through this loop. Your AI gets smarter. Your code gets better. Your workflow becomes unstoppable. Planning has two steps. First five planning. No structure here. Just you and your AI exploring ideas. Architecture, tech stack, concepts. You're getting on the same page about what you're building. This splits into two layers. Layer one, project planning done once per project. Your text stack, architecture decisions, coding conventions. Layer two, task planning. Done for every single feature. Analyzing your codebase, finding integration points, defining task specific rules. Step two, transforms your exploration into a structured plan. How? The four pillars of context engineering. [music] Memory, your conversation history from VIP planning. Rag, external documentation. You need to reference prompt engineering. Being explicit so AI makes zero assumptions. Task management. A clear checklist for step-by-step execution. Your plan should include goals, success criteria, documentation references, a task list, validation strategy, and desired code structure. This is your golden document. The more explicit, the fewer AI mistakes. Now, the fun part. Clear your conversation history. Start fresh. Reference your plan. Let AI execute task by task. Your mantra here, trust but verify. Watch loosely for correct tool calls. Make sure it's reading the right files. Check that it manages tasks properly. Verify its thinking tokens show it actually understands your plan. The structure is already there. Let it work. Validation is where you and AI work together. This is not optional. On the AI side, it runs unit tests, integration tests, linting. These should be built into your plan and execute automatically. On your side, you perform manual code review. Even if you are newer to coding, ask AI to explain changes you don't understand. Then run manual tests. Use your application like a real user would. Catch what automated tests miss. Here's where it gets exciting. The PIV loop isn't static. It evolves. On one end, you're manually prompting everything. on the other fully automated workflows where AI runs wild with systems you've designed. The spectrum moves through global rules then/comands then sub agents eventually you reach remote AI workflows kick off tasks from your phone while AI handles the entire PIV loop. The principles stay the same. The automation scales the building blocks that make this possible. Your AI layer global rules projectwide constraints done once updated rarely. They keep AI aligned with your architecture. SLC commands reusable workflow triggers, run planning, implementation or validation with a single command. Sub agents, specialized AI workers for specific tasks, code review, testing, documentation, each optimized for their role. This connect directly to every PI phase. This is just the foundation. === Cloud vs Self-Hosted? Here's the Real Trade-Off ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GasTL0DIehs

Today, I'm diving deep into the debate of self-hosting versus cloud for development, and I'm challenging myself to find out which one truly reigns supreme. But here's the thing. This isn't just another cloud is better or self-host everything argument. The answer might surprise you. Every developer faces this decision at some point, and most get it wrong the first time. Why? because they pick a side before understanding what actually matters. So, let's break this down together. By the end of this video, you'll know exactly how to make the right choice for your projects. The world of development is rapidly evolving, and one of the most critical decisions developers face today is where to host their projects. Whether it's self-hosting on your own servers or relying on cloud services, the choice can significantly impact the success of your project. As more businesses and individuals move online, understanding the pros and cons of each option has become increasingly important. Get this wrong and you could be bleeding money on infrastructure you don't need. Or worse, you could be exposing sensitive data because you chose convenience over security. The stakes are real. So, let's make sure you understand what's actually at play here. When choosing between self-hosting and cloud solutions, developers often find themselves in a dilemma. Cost is a significant factor. Self-hosting requires substantial upfront investment in hardware and infrastructure. Cloud services, on the other hand, offer a pay as you go model. Security is another major concern. Self-hosting allows for complete control over your data, but also places the burden of protection on your shoulders. Scalability comes into play, too. Cloud services often provide easy scalability but at a potentially higher cost. Then there's maintenance. With self-hosting, you're responsible for updates and repairs which can be timeconuming. Cloud providers usually handle maintenance but may have scheduled downtimes. Five factors, five trade-offs. Let's dig deeper into each side. Let's dive deeper into self-hosting. Self-hosting gives you full control over your data and applications. For instance, if you're working on a project that requires specific configurations or high levels of security, self-hosting might be the way to go. A good example is a company handling sensitive client information. They might prefer self-hosting to ensure maximum data protection. Think about it. Your data never leaves your premises. You control who accesses it. You decide how it's encrypted. You own the entire stack. For complianceheavy industries like healthcare or finance, this level of control isn't just nice to have. It's often required. And here's another advantage that's often overlooked. Predictable costs. Once you've made the initial investment, you know exactly what you're paying months over month. No surprise bills. However, self-hosting also means you're responsible for handling any issues that arise, which can be daunting. Server goes down at 3:00 a.m. That's on you. Security vulnerability discovered, you need to patch it. Hardware failure? Better have backups ready. You'll need in-house expertise or at least someone on call, system administrators, network engineers, security specialists. These aren't cheap. And time. So much time. Updating operating systems, monitoring for threats, managing certificates, troubleshooting network issues. For a small team or solo developer, this burden can be overwhelming. Every hour spent on infrastructure is an hour not spent building your product. It's a real trade-off you need to consider carefully. Cloud services, on the other hand, offer flexibility and scalability. They're perfect for projects that expect to grow rapidly or need to be accessible from anywhere in the world. Companies like Netflix and Dropbox rely heavily on cloud services to provide seamless experiences for their users. Netflix serves over 200 million subscribers globally. Could they do that with self-hosted infrastructure? Maybe. But the cloud makes it dramatically easier. The cloud gives you instant access to computing power that would take months to provision yourself. Need 100 servers for a product launch? Click a button. Done. Geographic distribution is built in. Your app can run in data centers across the globe, reducing latency for users everywhere. This is infrastructure that would cost millions to build yourself. Available on demand. The advantages of cloud services are compelling. Reduced upfront costs. No need to buy servers, racks, cooling systems, or lease data center space. You pay for what you use. Access to a wide range of tools and services. managed databases, machine learning platforms, content delivery networks, authentication services, the list goes on. Built-in redundancy and failover systems. If one server dies, another takes over automatically. Your users never notice. For a developer working on a project with stringent uptime requirements, cloud services might be more suitable due to these built-in reliability features. Automatic scaling means you handle traffic spikes without lifting a finger. Your app stays fast even when it goes viral. However, there are potential downsides. Dependence on internet connectivity is obvious. No internet, no access to your infrastructure. Security risks exist if not properly managed. Misconfigured permissions. Exposed storage buckets. These make headlines regularly. Costs can escalate quickly. That pay as you go model sounds great until you see the bill after a busy month or when you accidentally leave expensive instances running. Vendor login is real. Once you've built your entire system around one cloud provider's proprietary services, switching becomes painful and expensive, and you're trusting someone else with your data. For some businesses and some regulations, that's simply not acceptable. Know these risks before you commit. So, which one is better? It turns out the answer depends heavily on your specific needs. If you're working on a project that requires a high degree of customization and control, self-hosting might be the better option, you need specific hardware, you have strict compliance requirements, you want predictable costs at scale. On the other hand, if you're looking for scalability and flexibility, cloud services could be the way to go. You are a startup that might grow 10x next year. You need to move fast. You don't have infrastructure expertise inhouse. The best approach, be honest about your requirements. Make a list. What's non-negotiable? What's nice to have? Don't choose based on hype or what other companies do. Choose based on your actual situation. Consider a startup. They might begin with cloud services for easy scalability. Why? Because in the early days, you don't know if you'll have 100 users or 100,000. The cloud lets you start small and scale up. Pay only for what you need. Focus on building your product, not managing servers. But as they grow and require more control, they might switch to self-hosting. Dropbox famously did exactly this, moving from AWS to their own infrastructure and saving millions. This hybrid approach is often the smartest path. Cloud for experimentation self-hosting for stable, predictable workloads. Understanding your project's unique demands is crucial to making the right choice at the right time. In conclusion, choosing between self-hosting and cloud services is a critical decision. It depends on several factors: cost, security, scalability, maintenance, control. There's no universal right answer. What works for Netflix won't work for a local healthcare provider. What works for a startup won't work for a bank. By weighing these factors against your project specific needs, you can make an informed decision that sets your project up for success. Don't let anyone tell you one option is always better. The best choice is the one that aligns with your reality, your budget, your team, your requirements, your growth trajectory. That's the truth about self-hosting versus cloud. === Prompt Injection Just Got Scarier (Docker Has the Solution)  - Docker Sandboxes ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=h19RQv1qC00

You want to give your AI coding assistant full autonomy. Let it write code, run tests, install packages. But here's the thing. One prompt injection and that agent could steal your SSH keys, rid your credentials, exfiltrate your entire codebase. Scary, right? What if I told you there's a way to unleash your AI agent without risking your system? That's exactly what Docker Sandboxes give you. Docker Sandboxes runs your AI agents in isolated microVMs. Each sandbox gets its own Docker demon, its own file system, its own network stack. Your agent can install packages, spin up containers, even modify its environment. And none of it touches your host machine. One command gets you started. Docker sandbox run clawed then your project path. That's it. Full isolation in seconds. Traditional containers share the host kernel. That's great for performance, but it's a security risk for AI agents. Docker sandboxes takes a different approach. Each sandbox runs in a lightweight microVM with its own separate kernel. On Mac OS, it uses Apple's virtualization framework. On Windows, HyperV, real hypervisor level isolation, not just process boundaries. This means a compromised agent can't exploit kernel vulnerabilities to escape. The blast radius is fully contained. Here's what makes Docker sandboxes unique. Each sandbox gets its own private Docker demon. Your agent can pull images, spin up containers, build and test, all inside its isolated VM, but it can't see your host's containers. Can't access your host's Docker demon. Think of it like giving each agent its own private Docker desktop installation. Full capabilities, zero host access. Even if an agent goes rogue, your production containers stay untouched. Network isolation is critical for AI agents. You don't want a prompt injected agent phoning home to an attacker. Docker sandboxes enforces strict network boundaries. Sandboxes can't talk to each other. They can't access services on your host's local host. There's also an HTTP filtering proxy that can control which external endpoints agents reach. Configure policies to allow only the domains you trust. Data exfiltration blocked at the network layer. File system access is tightly controlled too. Only your workspace directory syncs between host and sandbox. The agent can read and write files there but nothing else on your system. Your ssh folder off limits. Your credentials invisible. Your other projects completely isolated. The sync is birectional. Changes you make on the host appear in the sandbox. Changes the agent makes sync back. Same absolute parts on both sides. No path confusion. This is defense and depth done right. Multiple isolation layers each catching what others might miss. PM isolation gives you a separate kernel. Network isolation blocks unauthorized connections. File system isolation protects your sensitive files. And the private Docker demon keeps agent containers invisible to your host. Even if an attacker finds a way through one layer, they hit another and another. That's how you give AI agents real autonomy safely. Here's how you actually use Docker sandboxes. Run Docker Sandbox run, then claude, then your project path. Your workspace syncs automatically at the same absolute path. Birectional, so changes flow both ways. need to debug or install tools. Use Docker sandbox exec with your sandbox name to get a bash shell inside. Each sandbox has its own private Docker demon. Agents only see containers they create, completely isolated from your host. When you're done, Docker Sandbox remove cleans everything up. Docker sandboxes isn't just for Claude. It supports multiple AI agents. Codeex, Gemini, Kira, and more are coming. Each sandbox is persistent. Installed packages survive restarts. Your workspace syncs automatically between host and sandbox at matching paths. To see your sandboxes, use docker sandbox ls, not regular docker ps. Ready to try it? One command docker sandbox run claw. Then your project path. You get full AI autonomy with real VM isolation. Your agent works freely. Your system stays protected. defense in depth that actually works because the agent that's truly isolated is the one you can trust with more. Now go build something amazing safely.

=== Is Gemini RAG the Easiest Way to Chat with PDFs? (3 Min Overview)  🤯 ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HDD9bB0kGjU

We've talked about rag retrieval augmented generation. It's the magic that lets AI chat with your own data. But let's be honest, building a rag pipeline from scratch is a pain. You need to parse PDFs, chunk text, generate embeddings, set up a vector database, and write retrieval logic. It's a lot of infrastructure just to ask a question about a document. Imagine the typical workflow. document to Python script to chunking to embedding model to vector DB to retrieval and finally to the LLM. That's six steps where things can break. What if you could just upload the file and ask a question? Enter Gemini file search. It's Google's fully managed rack system built directly into the API. No vector database to manage, no chunking algorithms to write. You just give it your files and it handles the rest. It's rag on easy mode. So what happens when you upload a file? You send your PDF, CSV or code file to the file search API. Google's infrastructure takes over. First, it passes and extracts text from complex formats. Then it chunks the text into logical pieces. Next, it converts those pieces into vectors using Google state-of-the-art models. And finally, it indexes them for high-speed retrieval. When you ask a question, the system automatically searches your uploaded files, retrieves the most relevant chunks, injects them into the model's context window, and generates an answer with citations. That last part is crucial. Unlike a standard LLM that might hallucinate, Gemini file search gives you receipts. It tells you exactly which part of your document it used to answer the question. Let's look at the code. It's shockingly simple. First, you upload your file using the media upload method. It handles huge files up to 2 million tokens of context in the model, but the store can hold much more. Next, you create a tool in the Gemini API. File search is just a tool you enable like function calling. You point it to your file set and you're done. Finally, you start a chat session. You don't need to manually retrieve context. You just send the users message, summarize the Q3 financial results. The model decides if it needs to look at the files, performs the search and answers. It's a gentic behavior out of the box. So, should you throw away your pine cone or VV8 setup? Not necessarily. Gemini file search is perfect for rapid prototyping internal tools where you don't want to manage infra and apps that need to chat with specific user uploaded documents on the fly. But you might still want a custom rack pipeline if you have billions of documents, need ultra specific chunking strategies, or need to run entirely on premise. Gemini search abstracts away the boring parts of Wreck. It lets you focus on the application, not the infrastructure. If you've been putting off building that chat with PDF feature because it seemed too hard, you no longer have an excuse. Go to Google AI Studio, upload a document, and see how fast you can build your own expert assistant. The code is in the description. Now, go build something.

=== What is Supabase? -  Explained in 5 Minutes! 🚀 ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=BBgq3t5V44Y

Let's talk about Superbase, the open-source backend platform that's quickly becoming the go-to choice for modern developers. If you've ever wished your back end could just handle itself, this is the tool you've been waiting for. Superbase is our backend as a service. That means it gives you production ready backend features right out of the box. A full Postgress database, authentication, serverless functions, file storage, all integrated, all instantly available and or without spinning up a single server yourself when using the official SAS offering. You can definitely host it yourself. See the link to my video above. Your front end simply connects to these services. If you've used Firebase before, this will look familiar. But Superbase isn't just a copy. It fixes one of the biggest limitations Firebase has the database. Superbase is built on Postgress, a powerful relational SQL database. You get structured tables, rows, and columns that scale beautifully. Firebase uses a NoSQL document system that's flexible, but can get messy fast. With Superbase, your data stays organized, queriable, and reliable from day one. Because it's SQL powered, the Superbase client uses clear SQL inspired commands. If you already know SQL, you'll feel right at home. And if you're new to it, that's fine, too. Superbase includes built-in AI helpers right inside the dashboard that can generate, explain, and correct your queries instantly. It makes learning CQL surprisingly smooth. But the real magic of Superbase goes beyond features. It's the philosophy. Superbase is entirely open- source. Every component, every service, every part of the platform. You can inspect the code, understand how it works, and even run the entire stack yourself. That means complete freedom, [music] complete transparency, and zero vendor lockin. To be honest, this is not fully true because certain feature like management dashboard and others are exclusive to the software as a service variant. With Firebase, your back end is locked to Google's infrastructure. With Superbase, you decide where everything runs, your own server, your preferred cloud provider, or of course, the official hosted Superbase platform. The flexibility is unmatched. So, how to get started? Most beginners start with a simple crude application. Create, read, update, and delete. These four actions teach you exactly how your app talks to the database. A basic example might be loading items from your database and displaying them in your user interface. Superbase handles every interaction through a clean, intuitive JavaScript client. add new items, update existing ones, delete old entries, everything syncs smoothly through the API, and yes, it works in real time if you enable it. And the best part, Superbase is fully framework agnostic. Whether you prefer React, View, Swelt, NextG, or just plain HTML and vanilla JavaScript, the workflow is identical. The client fits anywhere. To bring it all together, Superbased is an open-source backend as a service powered by Postgress. It gives you authentication, storage, serverless functions, real-time capabilities, and total deployment freedom. All in one unified developer experience, and also AI native functions like vector databases, which we did in cover in this quick overview. If you're ready to create your first Superbase project and connect your front end to it, jump into the next video where I show how to self-host Superbase. === Google A2A Takes AI Assistants to the NEXT LEVEL in 2025 ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QHOvZ5SSgsw

Imagine waking up tomorrow and your digital assistants finally started to work together instead of acting as separate islands. Your calendar automatically rescheduling when you are running late, your shopping list updating based on your receipts you saved, and your work apps collaborating without you lifting a single finger. This isn't science fiction. It's what Google's brand new agent to agent protocol offers you right now. In this video, I'm breaking down Google's A2A protocol, the new technology that's about to revolutionize how AI systems talk to each other. Whether you are a tech enthusiast or just curious about the future of AI, the next minutes I will show you why A2A might be the biggest AI breakthrough of 2025. So, what is A2A? In short, A2A stands for agent to agent. Think of an AI agent as a program bought or automated assistant designed to handle specific tasks like scheduling, searching, or analyzing data. Now, imagine you want your smart calendar, your project management tool, and maybe your sales app, all separate bots, to work together automatically so they can communicate to each other. That's what A2A enables. It's a standard protocol, kind of like a universal language that allows different AI agents, even ones that made from different companies to talk to each other to share information securely and collaborate to solve problems together in real time. And by the way, Google's A2A protocol does not replace MCP. It's designed to work alongside with entropics model context protocol in short MCP. While A2A connects different AI agents together so they can collaborate. MCP helps individual agents to access external tools and data. So together they make AI systems much more powerful and flexible. Why this is a big deal? Before A2A, connecting AI agents usually mean manually programming integrations. So each agent had to know exactly how the other agents work. That's a big pain and everything breaks easily when anything changes. A2A changes the game by letting agents discover each other's abilities on the fly and learn how to communicate with each other securely using a shared set of rules. Imagine you want to hire a new team member for your company. One AI agent can post the job. Another one finds and screens the applications. A third schedules the interviews. All these agents cooperate automatically even if they come from different companies because A2A connects them together. A2A is built around the concept of agent cards. An agent card is like a smart name tag for each AI agent, listing its capabilities and how others can interact with them. When, for example, agent A wants to work with agent B, it fetches agent B's card, just like getting instructions when meetings are new, all communication happens through a common open-source standard A2A protocol. So you could have a sales agent running on one cloud. A financial agent on the other hand built with any fancy framework in another cloud and an analytic agent hosted somewhere else. A2A lets them all communicate together regardless of how or where they are built and also very important A2A is as mentioned open source so anyone can build on it which is crucial for rapid technology growth and also allows everyone to check the code to see if it is secure. So what are the real world benefits and challenges? What's so powerful about A2A is not just the standard itself, but freedom and reliability. It makes it easier to add, upgrade, or replace agents without everything breaking since each agent dynamically learns about the others at runtime. But there are also some challenges we need to consider. The systems get more complex. Testing and security become trickier and different agents might distribute it all over the place, sort of like microservices. Google is already working on these issues and over time expect these systems to become more reliable and easier to use. So what is next for A2A and for you? A2A is still very new but dozens of big tech companies are already on board. So it could soon become a industry standard. Whether you are a developer, a tech enthusiast or a business owner, learning about A2A is in my view a smart move and keeps you up for the future. It represents a step towards the future where AI systems not only get smarter individually but also work together like a digital team to achieve bigger more complex tasks. That's it for this short introduction. If you like to see a deeper dive in this topic, take a look at Cole's video here where he explains it in more detail. And if you just want to start with your local AI development, building your own workflows and other stuff, take a look at my other videos here and get started. Thanks for watching and see you in the next one. Have a nice day. Bye.

=== WTF do all these AI Terms MEAN? ===
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=q_4Tsyyrq0g

Instead of being told exactly what to do in every situation, AI uses a set of rules called algorithms to learn from data and experience. This process often involves something called machine learning, which we will get into in a bit. Because it can learn, AI can get better over time, adapt to new informations, and handle complicated jobs. You can see AI in things like the smart personal assistant on your phone like Siri or Google. When you ask it a question, it understands your words, find the answers, and talks back to you. Another common example is how movie and music services suggest new things you might like based on what you've watched or listened before. Now that we just mentioned machine learning, what is that all about? Machine learning or ML is actually a sub field of AI. It focuses on teaching computers to learn from data without having to spell out every single step. Instead, the ML system finds patterns and examples and uses those patterns to make predictions and decisions. It's like learning by example, but the more data the system sees, the better it gets at a task. You can imagine trying to teach a computer to recognize pictures of cats. With traditional programming, you would have to write rules for every possible cat feature. That would take forever. But with machine learning, we feed the system loads of pictures labeled cat or not cat. The algorithm then adjusts its internal settings to correctly identify the patterns that make a cat a cat, like pointy ears or whiskers. Over time, it learns to recognize cats in new pictures, even if it's not seen them before. ML is really useful for tasks that are complex and happen repeatedly. Think about email spam. They learn to recognize junk mail by looking at tons of email marked as spam by the users. So it will work even when the spammers come up with new tricks and new email forms. Unluckily some spammers are also not that stupid. So they figure out new ways to spammer us. The question is how do these machine learning system actually learn? A lot of them use something called neural networks. Neural networks are a type of algorithm in machine learning that are inspired by how the human brain is structured. Think of them as layers of interconnected virtual norans or nodes. Each norin takes input, processes it, and passes the result to the next layer. Through this connections, neural networks can learn to recognize really complex patterns in data. Imagine these layers like a series of switches. The first layer takes raw data like the pixels of an image. The middle layer works on that data step by step finding important features and the final layer gives you a result like saying if the image is a cat or a dog. Noral networks learn by adjusting the strength of connections called weights between these neurons. If the network makes a mistake, it fine-tunes these weights to improve its future guesses. This is super powerful for things like image recognition. A neural network can learn to identify handwritten digits by seeing lots of examples. Now, let's take neural networks a step further with deep learning. Deep learning is basically machine learning that uses neural networks with many many layers. That's why it's called deep. Having lots of layers allows the computer to automatically discover very intricate patterns and features in large amount of data. It learns different levels of abstraction building up from simple features to more complex ones. You can think back to the cat example. A deep learning system might have one layer that that just detects edges. Another combines edges into shapes like eyes or ears and even deeper layers that recognize a whole catface. Because of this ability to analyze complex data, deep learning has led to big breakthroughs in AI, especially in areas like self-driving cars which need to interpret lots of sensor data and voice assistants like Alexa or Siri that might need to understand spoken language which can get very difficult because people have different accents. A lot of people are not not using English as their mother language but still use Siri or Alexa with this language. Deep learning models often need huge amount of data to learn effectively. Now let's talk about how AI understands and works with human language. That's where natural language processing or in short NLP comes in. NMP is a branch of AI that deals with the interaction between computers and our natural languages like English. It enables computers to understand, interpret and even generate human language in a meaningful way. NLP combines ideas from language study, linguistics, and machine learning. It involves breaking down languages into parts, understanding the meaning of the words and sentences and then potentially get generating language to respond or translate. NLP relies heavily on machine learning models that have been trained on vast amount of text to learn the patterns of a language. This allows things like virtual assistants, understanding your questions, language translations, services like Google translate and even spam filters that understand the language used in junk mails. You might have heard about things like Jet GPT recently. These are examples of large language models or LLMs. LM are super advanced AI systems trained on massive amount of text data. They learn the patterns, structure, and even some nuances of language, which allows them to generate text that sounds like very humanlike. Think of them as models that can predict the most likely next words in a sentence based on the text they've been trained on. LLMs use a type of neural networks that really good at processing sequences of text. Because they've seen so much text, they can assist with things like drafting emails, writing code, summarizing documents, and answering your questions in a conversional way. While they incredibly powerful, it's important to remember that they don't truly understand like human does. They're just very good at predicting words which come next. Examples of large language models are GPT4 and Gemini you may have heard and also more coding relevant models like entropic sonet 3.7 is the newest model and for jetp there are also reasoning models like 01 01 mini and even 03 at the moment. Maybe a quick fun fact, there is no O2 model from OpenAI because there's a company in Germany which is called O2 and this is a conflict. So pretty funny. So they directly uh switched to 03 from 01. Okay. Now let's talk about generative AI. This is where AI agents get really creative. Generative AI refers to AI systems that can create new content. This could be text, images, music, videos, or even computer code. So, source code for a web app you might have seen on my channel. Unlike AI that just analyzes existing data, generative AI produces original outputs that doesn't didn't exist before. Generative AI uses machine learning models that have learned the patterns of a training data set and can then produce new examples that follow those patterns. For example, large language models like we just talked about are are a form of generative AI for text. There are also other techniques like generative adversional networks for creating realistic images. I think a lot of you maybe tested this out with Jet GPT4 at the moment. You get very impressive results. There are also other tools or AIs like Midjourney for images and even ones for creating videos out of text. So yeah, you might have seen tools online where you can type a description and the AI generated generates an image based on that. That's generative AI in action. Artists are using these tools to create unique artwork and they can even help with things like designing new products or as I said implementing a web app for you or another software without knowing about programing programming at all. Sometimes though even the most knowledgeable AI models don't have all the answers especially when it comes to very specific or up-to-date informations. So they only have the the status of the trained data. So they don't know what happened today in the news. That's where retrieval augmented generation or in short rack comes in place. Rack is a technique that combines AI generative AI model with realtime information retal systems. Basically, when you ask the AI a question, it first looks up the relevant information from an external source like databases or just searching the internet with a browser and then uses that information to generate a more accurate and informed answer for you. It's like giving the eye an open book exam. Instead of just relying on what it learned during the training, it can search for and use the exact information it needs. This is super useful for things like customer support systems that need to give you the latest product informations or research assistants that need to refer to the most recent studies. Rack helps to make AI responses more reliable and reduces the chance of AI making things up. Now let's talk about AI agents. In the world of AI, an AI agent is a system that can act on its own to achieve a goal. Instead of waiting for single commands, an AI agent can make its own decision and take a series of actions to complete the task. It looks at its environment, decides what to do, and then takes action without needing step-by-step instructions for every move. You can think of it as an AI agent having a sense, think, and act cycle. It senses its environment or gets input. It thinks about what to do next based on its goals and then it acts to change something or get closer into its goals. Imagine telling an I agent to plan a dinner party for you. A capable agent could check your calendar, send invitations, search recipes, and even make a shopping list, all from that one request. AI agents are a step towards more interactive and self-sufficient AI. We've talked about how powerful AI models can be, especially large language models, but how do we actually get them to do what we want? That's where prompt engineering comes in. Prompt engineering is basically the skill of crafting the input text or instruction that you give to an AI model to get the best possible result because the AI generates its output based on the prompt it receives. How you ask a question or give it a task really affects the answer you get. For example, instead of just saying, "Tell me about dogs," a better prompt might be, "Give me in give me three interesting facts about dogs that would be suitable for a children's book." Being very specific and providing context helps the AI give you a more targeted answer. You can even ask the AI to take on a role or give it an example of the format you want. Often it's an iterative pro process. You might try a prompt, see the result, and then tweak your prompt to get closer what you need. Speaking of prompting, here is a more casual and AI assisted way of approaching programming called VIP coding. Instead of writing code line by line, with VIP coding, you describe what you want to code to do in plain language. and an AI coding assistant tries to generate the actual code for you. It's like explaining your idea to a really fast programmer who writes then the code. You won't get perfect results on the first go, but you can just iterate over it and tell the AI what to fix, what to add, and what doesn't work. So you might say something like I need a function that takes a list of numbers and returns the average and AI will try to write that code. Maybe you also want to provide the programming language for sure. So it has not to decide on its own in which language it should write this. You can then refue and refine the code and can ask even to make changes or fix errors. It's a more interactive way to code and it it can help people who don't have a lot of coding experience or coding experience at all to create simple programs. You just need to be aware that you have to to check everything. So it's not perfect. You are so at the moment I would say it's more like a proof of concept. you can test things out or even write programs that are not need to be perfect so they are not in an enterprise environment and you maybe use them for your private stuff at your home. Finally, it's important to be aware of something called hallucination in AI context. This doesn't mean the AI is seeing things, but rather it refers to when an AI system generates an output that is incorrect or completely made up, but it presents it confidently in a way that is true. It's like the AI is guessing and sounding very sure of itself, even if it's wrong. This can happen because AI models learn patterns from data, but they don't have a true understanding of the world and the data. If they don't have the correct information, they might try to fill in the blanks with something that sounds plausible. For example, if you ask an AI who wrote a certain book, it might confidently give you the wrong author. So it's crucial to remember that AI isn't always right and it's always a good idea to doublech checkck important informations. Techniques like rack are helping to reduce the hallucinations by grounding answers in real world data. So there you have it for today. A beginner's guide to some of the key concepts in the exciting world of AI. We've covered a lot from the basics of what is AI, how it learns, how it understands language, how it creates or can create new content, and even some of the challenges it brings. Remember, AI is constantly evolving, so keep exploring and learning. So, there's nearly every day new stuff around AI. So, if you found this video helpful, give a like. And if you also wonder what MCP is, take a look at this video.

But this is not included in the default workflows right now. So in this tutorial, I want to show you how to use sear xng, which is an alternative to brave search.
